  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2396413289801276356">This haunting, suspenseful tale of love and vengeance by the author of the international bestseller <i>Silk</i> surges with the hypnotic power of the ocean sea. <br/><br/>In <i>Ocean Sea</i>, Alessandro Baricco presents a hypnotizing postmodern fable of human malady--psychological, existential, erotic--and the sea as a means of deliverance. At the Almayer Inn, a remote shoreline hotel, an artist </span>
  <span id="freeText2396413289801276356" style="display:none">This haunting, suspenseful tale of love and vengeance by the author of the international bestseller <i>Silk</i> surges with the hypnotic power of the ocean sea. <br /><br />In <i>Ocean Sea</i>, Alessandro Baricco presents a hypnotizing postmodern fable of human malady--psychological, existential, erotic--and the sea as a means of deliverance. At the Almayer Inn, a remote shoreline hotel, an artist dips his brush in a cup of ocean water to paint a portrait of the sea. A scientist pens love letters to a woman he has yet to meet. An adulteress searches for relief from her proclivity to fall in love. And a sixteen-year-old girl seeks a cure from a mysterious condition which science has failed to remedy. When these people meet, their fates begin to interact as if by design. Enter a mighty tempest and a ghostly mariner with a thirst for vengeance, and the Inn becomes a place where destiny and desire battle for the upper hand. Playful, provocative, and ultimately profound, Ocean Sea is a novel of striking originality and wisdom.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2396413289801276356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17995662270790896079">When I started reading it, I was slightly drunk and my first reaction was, what the f*ck. But now I can add without any exaggeration that Ocean Sea is one of the best books I have ever read. <br/><br/>7 people, Ocean Sea (it deserves capital letters) and Baricco who put literature on higher level. It is possibly the weirdest book I ever had in my hands and it's a book with the best description of life. No, don’t be fooled with popular psychology genre, there is no instant answer and you have to massage yo</span>
  <span id="freeText17995662270790896079" style="display:none">When I started reading it, I was slightly drunk and my first reaction was, what the f*ck. But now I can add without any exaggeration that Ocean Sea is one of the best books I have ever read. <br /><br />7 people, Ocean Sea (it deserves capital letters) and Baricco who put literature on higher level. It is possibly the weirdest book I ever had in my hands and it's a book with the best description of life. No, don’t be fooled with popular psychology genre, there is no instant answer and you have to massage your brain to perceive everything it offers. <br /><br />First I couldn’t relate with his poetry and sentences that were overloaded with adjectives, but I googled a little bit, and discovered that Baricco was an opera critic. <br /><br />It is<br />a<br />poem<br />with the sound<br />of sea<br />written as a <br />prose. <br /><br />What he did is rare. There is this palette of characters: young Elisewin with mysterious sickness, father Pluche with beautiful prayers, adulteress Ann Deveria, painter Plasson, scientist Bartleboom, doctor Savigny and sailor Adams – all of them being desperate and in search for something. Water, sea, ocean has the power to heal.<br /><br />And I can understand that perfectly as I run to the shore every time when I need to clear my mind. Sea has always been the healer: physical, emotional and psychological. <br /><br />Croatia and Italy share the same sea – Adriatic. And being raised on that water, even if it’s not the ocean, even if I know that he didn’t place Almayer anywhere near... I felt utterly connected with this book.<br /><br />This is the first time that I’ve been so deep in poetic salt. <br /><br />I love difficult literature, and this one was sometimes overwhelming and I forced myself to read it slowly. You can easily skip many insights if you are not paying attention. Because of this, it is certain that I will read this book again. <br /><br />It's not easy at all to describe it. Style of writing is original, every character has its own way of structured words, and then you have so many layers of meaning which are connected with metaphors and above all - amazingly described emotions. <br /><br />Unbelievably beautiful book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17995662270790896079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_88882419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/88882419" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/94602-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521304176p2/94602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/88882419?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/94602-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=the-continent">the-continent</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=romantical">romantical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88882419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8223790787080877946">There was about a minute when I was a little girl when I wanted to play baseball with the boys, and my dad was trying to teach me some stuff. I was about five when this happened, but I remember one of the first things he said to me was that, “You can’t always swing for the fences.” Which was good advice, because not only did I miss the ball most of the time, but given the size and the velocity at which I swung the bat, more often than not I ended up flat on my butt. But the boys were watching an</span>
  <span id="freeText8223790787080877946" style="display:none">There was about a minute when I was a little girl when I wanted to play baseball with the boys, and my dad was trying to teach me some stuff. I was about five when this happened, but I remember one of the first things he said to me was that, “You can’t always swing for the fences.” Which was good advice, because not only did I miss the ball most of the time, but given the size and the velocity at which I swung the bat, more often than not I ended up flat on my butt. But the boys were watching and I wanted to beat them, so I swung for the fences anyway. Cycle, rinse, repeat.<br /><br />In the prose and subject matter of <i>Ocean Sea</i> Baricco seems to have adopted a somewhat similar mentality. Granted his goal is probably a bit more noble. Rather than beating neighborhood boys, he wants to provide a meditation on the idea of the sea, what it has meant, what it used to mean and what it has become, and most importantly, what possibilities it holds for people who need it. Given that I’ve just spent a semester reading about the Mediterranean, the <i>Mare Nostrum</i>, the “Middle Sea,” the fault line of conflicts for a thousand years, and now possibly the most overdetermined space in the history of ever, I was interested to hear what he had to say. I think possibly his best idea, which he didn’t spend enough time on, is how the meaning of oceans changed in the 18th and 19th century. Their original status- and of course we must keep in mind that I’m talking about status among Europeans- was as threatening, Other spaces where monsters dwelled and Satan ruled, but when the English started an empire that was based on the sea, it could not be a “free space” given over to pirates any longer. No, the sea had to be made something respectable, where proper Englishmen could (and should) go to find glory and make a living. The sea had to be <i>tamed</i>, in other words. And what better way to do this than by allowing, nay, advising women to go to the sea- in fact having medical doctors say that the sea was <i>healthy</i> for you? If this was a space where weak women whose honor must be protected could go (though of course with prescribed limits), well then who could not approve of it? Baricco has a wonderful few pages where he describes women at the sea, and in particular the curtained “sea baths” that allowed them to take part in its healing properties while staying within respectable boundaries: <br /><br /><i>“women. The sea suddenly seemed to have been waiting for them forever. To listen to the doctors, it had been there, for millennia, patiently perfecting itself, with the sole and precise intention of offering itself as a miraculous unguent for their afflictions of body and soul. Just as, while sipping tea in impeccable drawing rooms, impeccable doctors-weighing their words well in order to explain with paradoxical courtesy- would tell impeccable husbands and fathers over and over that the disgust for the sea, and the shock and the terror was in reality a seraphic cure for sterility, anorexia, nervous exhaustion, overexcitement, menopause, anxiety and insomnia. An ideal experience inasmuch as it was a remedy for the ferments of youth and a preparation for wifely duties. A solemn baptism for young ladies to become women…<br /><br />… we could think of a woman-respected loved, mother, woman. For whatever reason-illness- brought to a sea that she would otherwise never have seen… Her hair hangs loose and she is barefoot and this is not a mere detail, it is absurd, along with that little white tunic and the trousers that leave her ankles exposed, you could imagine her slim hips, it is absurd, only her boudoir has seen her like this, and yet, like that, there she is on an enormous beach, where there is no one of the viscous, stagnant air of the bridal bed, but the gusty sea breeze, bearing the edict of a wild freedom removed… and it is clear: she cannot feel it…<br /><br />…They were <b>drinking</b> the sea water, things had gone that far, the water that until the day before had been horror and disgust, and the privilege of a forlorn and barbarous humanity… Now they were <i>sipping</i> it, those same divine invalids who waled along the water's edge imperceptibly dragging one leg... It was the same world as ever that had suddenly been transferred, for wholly medical purposes, to the edge of an abyss abhorred for centuries and now chosen as the promenade of suffering... The wave bath the doctors called it… a kind of patented sedan chair for getting into the sea, it was for the ladies obviously <b>to protect them from indiscreet eyes</b>. … Only science can do certain things, this is the truth. To sweep away centuries of disgust- the horrendous sea womb of corruption and death- and invent that idyll little by little spreading to all the beaches of the world…”</i><br /><br />I liked this part. I think he's right about a lot. I loved the way that Baricco wrote about the sea as a negotiation between civilization and freedom, and saints and madmen, between tradition and the possibilities of the future, as a place where things are erased, where there can by definition be no firm foundations, where everything changes and nothing does, where time seems to stop because of its very repetitiveness. I think Baricco is getting at an argument that is only now coming to the fore in the many studies of the sea that have been released in the last few years. Maybe it is fitting that it took an Italian- an Italian who <i>would</i> include a priest in his cast of characters, to do it. <br /><br />However, the unfortunate part is that he didn’t really live up to these ideas. The swinging for the fences prose, first of all. The part I quoted above was probably the best part, and I liked it. But the vast majority of it… he was going for poetry, that much was obvious. Unfortunately, when you go for that in print, I think that you have to go about it in a particular way. Either you’re Nabokov and you can do what the hell you want, or you have to mix it in with other things. Your metaphor may be flowery or unusual, but you must surround it with plain or unobtrusive prose that leads up to it and away from it. And most of all, there must be a point to it. For example, at one point, Baricco writes: <i> “Only after a little did the gardener’s footsteps engrave themselves on the silence.”</i> First of all, I think I wrote that when I was 15. Second of all, the metaphorical word choice there serves absolutely no purpose except to show me that Baricco spent far too much time thinking of a better way to say, “he walked away.” Thirdly, shut up. There was also another pretty unbearable sex scene where he uses the structure of the inexorable waves to explain the different reasons why a young girl and a middle aged man would want to have sex with each other. Now <i>there’s</i> a subject that one more man definitely needed to rhapsodize badly about (“<i>sighs, sighs in Elisewin’s throat-soaring velvet, sighs at each new step in that world that crosses mountains never seen and lakes with forms unimaginable…whoever would have said that by kissing the eyes of a man you could see so far away- by caressing the legs of a young girl you could run so fast and escape-escape from everything to see so far away…”</i> VOMIT. If you’re going for the oldest subject in the book you better bring it, man). I could go on, but I think you get the idea. Overwrought, overly dramatic, and once he’s finished expressing his few ideas about the sea, all he’s got left is filling in the blanks by repeating himself in more and more fanciful ways.<br /><br />I also thought the structure was both weird and off putting. It starts off with a section that is half straightforward prose, half impressionistic, but leans strongly towards the impressionism by the end of it. He’s big on ellipses, on not identifying speakers, on not finishing sentences and wise children who can read your mind (VOMIT, again). They volley minimalistic profundity back and forth to each other and almost never reach a place that goes deeper than that- with few exceptions. The ocean bit I talked about above, and there’s another lovely bit about Elisewin’s father arranging her journey to the sea. I liked when he started to veer into fully formed fairy tale, I hated when he tried to turn his prose into poetry. In a wincingly literal fashion at times- one or a few words per line, stopping paragraphs and starting them in the middle of the next, etc. Ugh. Didn’t we leave this novelist poet envy back in the 18th century? There was a part that ALMOST worked with a priest who wrote unusual and specific prayers (“A Prayer for a Little Boy Who Cannot Say the Letter R,” “Prayer for a Man Who Is Falling into a Ravine” –“obviously it is very short”-), but then he felt the need to write out the prayers rather than just tell me the wonderful titles and let me imagine it for myself. And unfortunately, the execution did not live up to the imagination. As with most of this book.<br /><br />The next section was stream of consciousness, set up in a mockingly twelve days of Christmas sort of incantation, with a conflict that’s clearly drawn from the shipwreck that inspired <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:G%C3%A9ricault_-_La_zattera_della_Medusa.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">this Gericault painting</a> and a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Raft_of_the_Medusa" rel="nofollow noopener">scandal that became an enduring part of French history</a>. But the conflict set up because of it was obvious and banal. I hate this thing writers do where they put their otherwise flimsy tales into the context of some great historical event- World War II stories of any kind, all those Regency romance writers who use the Napoleonic wars- to try to give their stories some sort of weight and get prizes. Using the shield of historical events that are virtually off limits to criticism to block criticism of your writing by giving you some sort of impenetrable moral superiority is pretty cowardly and lazy. I don’t mind the use of big events, but either tell me something more interesting than “Hitler is bad,” or write your book in such a way that, “Hitler is bad,” seems new again. Baricco did not accomplish this. Which meant that his closing section, which was essentially an extended epilogue, had no emotional impact at all (except for perhaps the chapter about the “seductress”- as the book jacket calls her- who wrote a long letter to her lover begging for the freedom to be by herself. Which of course, he ignores. And THAT ends super well. Argh, MEN.) And then, on top of all that, as if this whole exercise weren’t already incredibly self-indulgent and pointless, he felt the need to insert himself into the last chapter for no reason whatsoever? Other than to establish.. what? He wrote this by the sea? He is finished contemplating the sea? Yes I saw that there were only a few pages left. Why did we need this announcement? It’s hardly heart rending when you saw to it yourself that most of the characters were vague and representative rather than fully formed humans- in fact at every point where they seemed almost human, where I almost formed a picture of someone I knew, you stopped abruptly and wrote another terrible poem. <br /><br />You know what this was like? It was like someone deliberately trying to write a book consisting only of things he doesn’t know very much about, in styles he’s not very good at. Every time something natural to him came out, he quashed it, ruthlessly. Was this book an experiment of some kind? A punishment? Why would he keep swinging for something and landing on his butt? I don’t know, but in the end, I think that Baricco should have let himself breathe a bit. I think we all would have enjoyed ourselves much more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8223790787080877946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_180504266" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180504266" />
    <a title="Edita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5685453-edita"><img alt="Edita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595755309p2/5685453.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180504266?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edita" href="/user/show/5685453-edita">Edita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5685453-edita?shelf=alessandro-baricco">alessandro-baricco</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180504266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8240114117570762524">Sand as far as the eye can see, between the last hills and the sea— the sea—in the cold air of an afternoon almost past, and blessed by the wind that always blows from the north.<br/>The beach. And the sea.<br/>It could be perfection— an image for divine eyes— a world that happens, that is all, the mute existence of land and water, a work perfectly accomplished, truth—but once again it is the redeeming grain of a man that jams the mechanism of that paradise, a trifle capable on its own of suspending all t</span>
  <span id="freeText8240114117570762524" style="display:none">Sand as far as the eye can see, between the last hills and the sea— the sea—in the cold air of an afternoon almost past, and blessed by the wind that always blows from the north.<br />The beach. And the sea.<br />It could be perfection— an image for divine eyes— a world that happens, that is all, the mute existence of land and water, a work perfectly accomplished, truth—but once again it is the redeeming grain of a man that jams the mechanism of that paradise, a trifle capable on its own of suspending all that great apparatus of inexorable truth, a mere nothing, but one planted in the sand, an imperceptible tear in the surface of that sacred icon, a minuscule exception come to rest on the perfection of that boundless beach. From afar he would be no more than a black dot: amid nothingness, the nothing of a man and a painter’s easel.<br />The easel is anchored by slender cords to four stones placed on the sand. It sways imperceptibly in the wind that always blows from the north. The man is wearing waders and a large fisherman’s jacket . He is standing, facing the sea, twirling a slim paintbrush between his fingers. On the easel, a canvas.<br />He is like a sentinel— this you must realize—standing there to defend that part of the world from the silent invasion of perfection, a small crack that fragments that spectacular stage set of being. As it is always like this, you need only the glimmer of a man to wound the repose of that which would otherwise be a split second away from becoming truth, but instead immediately becomes suspense and doubt once more, because of the simple and infinite power of that man, who is a slit, a chink, a small doorway through which return a flood of stories and the enormous inventory of what of what could be, an infinite gash , a marvelous wound,a path made of thousands of steps where nothing can be true anymore but everything will be—just as the steps are of that woman who, wrapped up in a purple cloak, her head covered, is pacing the beach with measured tread, skirting the backwash of of the sea, her feet tracing furrows from right to left across what is by then the lost perfection of the great picture , consuming the distance that separates her from the man until she comes to within a few paces of him, and then right beside him, where it takes nothing to pause and silently look on.<br />The man does not even turn. He continues staring out at the sea. Silence. From time to time he dips the brush in a copper cup and makes a few light strokes on the canvas. In their wake the bristles of the brush leave a shadow of the palest obscurity that the wind immediately dries, bringing the pristine white back to the surface. Water. In the copper cup there is only water. And on the canvas, nothing. Nothing that may be seen.<br />The north wind blows as it always does, and the woman pulls her purple cloak closer around her.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8240114117570762524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1118946229" />
    <a title="Asghar Abbas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37421322-asghar-abbas"><img alt="Asghar Abbas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444319622p2/37421322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1118946229?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Asghar Abbas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Asghar Abbas" href="/user/show/37421322-asghar-abbas">Asghar Abbas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1118946229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4582701560717615597"><br/>Let me Drown in this gorgeous book. <br/><br/>One of the first books I read with an overall poetic prose, a speculative fiction of sorts. It's been years, decades, lifetime maybe, but I feel like I'm still adrift in that ocean.</span>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1405967-lavinia?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1266595975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15826167202080599533">And the award for getting me out of my comfort zone goes to Alessandro Baricco! I have never read anything written by him (and I’m not sure I will in the near future) but this little thing was like a breath of (somewhat) fresh air. Yes, yes, yes, it’s about the sea, the overwhelming, powerful, healing, cruel, regenerating etc. sea. And about the not so original idea of bringing a bunch of unknown, totally different people together. And how the sea is their friend or nemesis, how it all starts (w</span>
  <span id="freeText15826167202080599533" style="display:none">And the award for getting me out of my comfort zone goes to Alessandro Baricco! I have never read anything written by him (and I’m not sure I will in the near future) but this little thing was like a breath of (somewhat) fresh air. Yes, yes, yes, it’s about the sea, the overwhelming, powerful, healing, cruel, regenerating etc. sea. And about the not so original idea of bringing a bunch of unknown, totally different people together. And how the sea is their friend or nemesis, how it all starts (where, Plasson?) and ends (where, Bartleboom?) with it.<br /><br />Sometimes I felt I was eating a leftover salad with different things thrown in the mix (theater? poetry? stream of consciousness?) and I was trying to identify which is which, separate potatoes from onions and olives, but then again, a salad is a salad, it’s better not to overthink it. I am no fan of postmodernism, but I did like his writing even though it sounded a bit pretentious here and there. Oh, and that particular episode with Bartleboom trying to find the woman of his dreams, that was the funniest thing I’ve read in years!<br /><br />(And yes, I went to the sea while reading the book and I thought about it, how cheesy is that?) </span>
  <a data-text-id="15826167202080599533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1352037481" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17252825782159482519">There is something almost hypnotic about this book, drawing the reader into its setting, into the sea. The writing is lyrical and lovely and I was impressed by the depth the author was able to achieve in under 250 pages, especially considering the multiple interwoven storylines. I felt fully invested in each of the character's narratives and each of the characters felt very real, even with the somewhat ethereal feel of the book as a whole. A truly masterful work. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_439455490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/439455490" />
    <a title="Mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9671216-mia"><img alt="Mia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528672682p2/9671216.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9671216-mia?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9671216-mia?shelf=fantasy-and-supernatural">fantasy-and-supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9671216-mia?shelf=romance">romance</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer439455490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2113111065229456930">This is one of those books I always return to - be it I'm feeling a tad melancholic, or happy-beyond-words, or sad-beyond-a-doubt. <br/><br/>Baricco's lyrical style and the ease in which his sentences flow is very similar to the river and the metaphor of the ocean/sea he uses in this work (and others). You feel kind of lulled by the language, taken on a slow, magical journey - and the emotions it invokes are also as diverse and as "soft". I don't know how better to describe it, but this book has so much b</span>
  <span id="freeText2113111065229456930" style="display:none">This is one of those books I always return to - be it I'm feeling a tad melancholic, or happy-beyond-words, or sad-beyond-a-doubt. <br /><br />Baricco's lyrical style and the ease in which his sentences flow is very similar to the river and the metaphor of the ocean/sea he uses in this work (and others). You feel kind of lulled by the language, taken on a slow, magical journey - and the emotions it invokes are also as diverse and as "soft". I don't know how better to describe it, but this book has so much beauty it's simply overwhelming. I understand this may not appeal to some readers and there are times when his imagery and descriptions could be said to be a bit self-indulgent, but I didn't really mind these one or two moments because on the whole, the book was stunning. <br /><br />The story itself (stories, that is) is also dream-like and sort of airy, there isn't a lot that happens per se, not in the usual sense. For me, it mostly felt like reading someone's letters (and in some cases, that was the case), or diary, or just seeing flashbacks from a person's past. The book has a very surreal quality to it, and as such, I think it should be regarded more as poetry or a painting because it invokes that kind of a sentiment - that of a brilliant piece of art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2113111065229456930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15765202692715556782">One of my fave books ever. Baricco, you either love him or hate him, there's no inbetween. <br/><br/>I don't know how this particular edition (I own the original, in Italian), so I can't really vouch for the translation, but the Italian version caught my breath away. You laugh and you cry, you smile and you hurt, and smile again. The beginning is confusing because of Baricco's peculiar writing style *and* the plot... well, let's face it, the plot *is* weird, but it's a good kind of weird imho. Just give t</span>
  <span id="freeText15765202692715556782" style="display:none">One of my fave books ever. Baricco, you either love him or hate him, there's no inbetween. <br /><br />I don't know how this particular edition (I own the original, in Italian), so I can't really vouch for the translation, but the Italian version caught my breath away. You laugh and you cry, you smile and you hurt, and smile again. The beginning is confusing because of Baricco's peculiar writing style *and* the plot... well, let's face it, the plot *is* weird, but it's a good kind of weird imho. Just give this novel and Baricco a chance, if you happen to go "huh?" while reading the first 50 pages. You won't regret it.<br /><br />The imagery is *stunning*, btw. It's like a fairy tale and yet it is not. Oh definately not. There's romance and drama, but it has very dark pages! </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15250912-elena?shelf=by-the-water">by-the-water</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer586094717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10735758528688390629">Imagine the most perfect bite of the most decadent dish melting in your mouth and giving you indescribable pleasure.... Imagine the most harmonic melody caressing your ear... Imagine a picture so perfect that you find yourself in it... This is the kind of prose you have the pleasure devouring!<br/><br/>"Ocean Sea" is a picture painted with words, that has its own melody to it. It's the finest poetry in form of a novel. The main character here is The Sea - doctor, killer, God... Every now and then people d</span>
  <span id="freeText10735758528688390629" style="display:none">Imagine the most perfect bite of the most decadent dish melting in your mouth and giving you indescribable pleasure.... Imagine the most harmonic melody caressing your ear... Imagine a picture so perfect that you find yourself in it... This is the kind of prose you have the pleasure devouring!<br /><br />"Ocean Sea" is a picture painted with words, that has its own melody to it. It's the finest poetry in form of a novel. The main character here is The Sea - doctor, killer, God... Every now and then people disturb its perfection but that doesn't bother The Sea - people come and go, but The Sea... it's here, always was, always will be, nothing will overshadow its magnificence! <br /><br />There is not much more I can say any better than Alessandro Baricco. I loved this book so much I would give it six stars if I could. Highly recommended for everybody who enjoys fine art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10735758528688390629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8117799754475447965">What a dream like experience! What a surreal narrative! I actually did not expect it to have some sort of plot, which it did and also didn't at the same time. It was about that little "Almajero"tavern and its temporary population. That's all there is to know. What fallows are surreal dream like conversations, visions, dreams, magic, sighs and paintings. Prayers and infidelities, revenges and loses of virginity and innocence.   <span id="freeText8117799754475447965" style="display:none">What a dream like experience! What a surreal narrative! I actually did not expect it to have some sort of plot, which it did and also didn't at the same time. It was about that little "Almajero"tavern and its temporary population. That's all there is to know. What fallows are surreal dream like conversations, visions, dreams, magic, sighs and paintings. Prayers and infidelities, revenges and loses of virginity and innocence. And sea. Sea. Sea. Where are its eyes? Where are its borders? And why does it call you? <br />A book like that needs more than one reading, and I feel like I might revisit it in the future, with different set of mind. I feel like this is that ind of book, where you read it and every time you find something new, something you haven't seen before. <br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2267763436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2267763436" />
    <a title="Aušrinė" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5084456-au-rin"><img alt="Aušrinė" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515863441p2/5084456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2267763436?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aušrinė" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aušrinė" href="/user/show/5084456-au-rin">Aušrinė</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670748" href="/book/show/3069455-ocean-sea">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Aušrinė by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/21011589-michela-prete">Michela Prete</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5084456-au-rin?shelf=home-library">home-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5084456-au-rin?shelf=2018-i%C5%A1%C5%A1%C5%ABkis">2018-iššūkis</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2267763436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer291751514352496785">“<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/208631.Ocean_Sea' title='Ocean Sea by Alessandro Baricco' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ocean Sea</a>” was recommended to me by fellow PhD student from university I was visiting. And I was very surprised when on the last day of my visit she gave me this book as a gift. This is one of the very few books gifted to me not by my family members and without me asking for it. Thank you, Michela!<br/><br/>Before starting this book, I didn’t have any expectations, only some examples from the plot. But I was asked to give the impression about it when I finish. So I started comparing it to something I hav</span>
  <span id="freeText291751514352496785" style="display:none">“<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/208631.Ocean_Sea" title="Ocean Sea by Alessandro Baricco" rel="nofollow noopener">Ocean Sea</a>” was recommended to me by fellow PhD student from university I was visiting. And I was very surprised when on the last day of my visit she gave me this book as a gift. This is one of the very few books gifted to me not by my family members and without me asking for it. Thank you, Michela!<br /><br />Before starting this book, I didn’t have any expectations, only some examples from the plot. But I was asked to give the impression about it when I finish. So I started comparing it to something I have read before. First thing to come in my mind was the setting. An inn on the seashore. It reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/24197.Michael_Koryta" title="Michael Koryta" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Koryta</a> “<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8585317.The_Cypress_House" title="The Cypress House by Michael Koryta" rel="nofollow noopener">The Cypress House</a>”. And this is the end of similarities of these two books, because the stories cannot be more different.<br /><br />Then I started to look for comparison of a writing style. But before that I would like to talk about style of the book in general. I was told that in Italian it is really beautiful, but this might not be translated equally well into English. But I loved how <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15147790.Alastair_McEwan" title="Alastair McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">Alastair McEwan</a> translated <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/908.Alessandro_Baricco" title="Alessandro Baricco" rel="nofollow noopener">Alessandro Baricco</a>. Actually, this is the first time when I felt that English language can be organic or flowy. I am not sure how to best describe it, but it is simply beautiful. It defies a lot of grammatical rules and, if written in “MS Word”, half of the sentences would be underlined in green - <i>Fragment (consider revising)</i>. But that’s that makes it so easy to read for me. Maybe it reminds me of Lithuanian language, which has more freedom in the order of the words in the sentence. In this book, a lot of sentences were written from the end to beginning compared to normal sentences. I used to think that English is very strict and doesn’t have much artistic freedom. But this book is complete opposite. Also, the words are very simple and the stories are very simple, but somehow it still doesn’t feel real. The simplicity of writing style and topics reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3354.Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">Haruki Murakami</a>. But there are subtle differences between these two authors. It took me awhile to find the correct words for it, but I think I managed it. Haruki Murakami writes more surreal things, while Alessandro Baricco – ethereal. Really, I cannot think of any other word to describe it. It’s like seeing something, trying to reach that thing, but not being able to. If you have no idea what I am talking about, just read the book – it is worth it.<br /><br />That I described earlier is mostly in the first part of the book and I liked it the most. Here the reader meets all the characters while they arrive at the Almayer Inn: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a painter, who paints sea with sea; a scientist, who tries to find the end of the sea; a woman, who seeks solitude from her husband and from her lover; a girl, who has a mysterious fear; a priest, who protects the girl and writes poems; a stranger, who doesn’t like to be noticed; a few children, who run the inn <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. In second part reader follows the event at sea told by two different people. This part is very different from the first one, it even shows some cruelty. At first it looks like it is not related to any of the previous stories. But everything becomes clear in the third part. Here every chapter is dedicated to different character and reader learns about their destiny. And every chapter brings different emotions: happiness, laughter, sadness, astonishment, interest and everything in between.<br /><br />I am really glad that this book was recommended to me. And I think it won’t be the last one I read from Alessandro Baricco.</span>
  <a data-text-id="291751514352496785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18198429381768015046">Baricco was the first writer to taught me how to read books as poetry.   <span id="freeText18198429381768015046" style="display:none">Baricco was the first writer to taught me how to read books as poetry. The characters come from some different world where nostalgia is present, pointless efforts are the most logical thing that one can think of and pain has a healing sense. Overwhelming is the only word I can think of to start to describe it, but to finish it would take too much so I'll just, predictably, finish with a quote:<br /><br />"Perhaps sometimes life shows you a side of itself which leaves you with nothing more to say"</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9651166009616310480">The time is è indefinite. The space is the Almayer Inn that is on the beach of an unspecified sea. It has seven rooms and mysteriously emphatic kids run it. It hosts Plasson the painter, Bartleboom the scientist, the beautiful Madame Daviera, the preist Padre Pucle who accompanies the young aristocratic and hypersensitive Elisewin, the mysterious Adams aka Thomas, the doctor Savigny and their stories. <br/>“I meant to say that I want it, life, I would do anything to have it all, all that there is, so</span>
  <span id="freeText9651166009616310480" style="display:none">The time is è indefinite. The space is the Almayer Inn that is on the beach of an unspecified sea. It has seven rooms and mysteriously emphatic kids run it. It hosts Plasson the painter, Bartleboom the scientist, the beautiful Madame Daviera, the preist Padre Pucle who accompanies the young aristocratic and hypersensitive Elisewin, the mysterious Adams aka Thomas, the doctor Savigny and their stories. <br />“I meant to say that I want it, life, I would do anything to have it all, all that there is, so much <br />to go crazy, it doesn’t matter, I can also go insane but life I won’t miss it, I want it, indeed, though it would hurt like hell to live is what I crave for. I’ll make it, won’t I?”<br />Elisewin, a shy and hypersensitive girl, utters these words with a solemnity about which she was completely unaware: one could imagine her as she starts nervously to state her thoughts, getting more and more aware about the importance that those words could acquire for every <br />character of the story. These words seem like defining the items of a collective unconscious that finds its way towards the characters’ awareness introducing the key element that will lead to their catharsis: the sea.</span>

<div id="review_186299938" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186299938" />
    <a title="Courtney Johnston" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3209040-courtney-johnston"><img alt="Courtney Johnston" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1264558613p2/3209040.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/186299938?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Courtney Johnston" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Courtney Johnston" href="/user/show/3209040-courtney-johnston">Courtney Johnston</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186299938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15132079387142348237">I would call 'Ocean Sea' a fable, but generally a fable has a plot, a reveal and a message. So perhaps 'Ocean Sea' is a confection, a collection of conceits and meditations and character sketches, based on the idea of the sea and the different things it has meant over the centuries.<br/><br/>A motley collection of characters come together at the mysterious Almayer Inn, a hotel on the sea front staffed by a family of children, Dira and her four brothers. <br/><br/><blockquote>Perched on the last narrow edge of the world, a ston</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15132079387142348237" style="display:none">I would call 'Ocean Sea' a fable, but generally a fable has a plot, a reveal and a message. So perhaps 'Ocean Sea' is a confection, a collection of conceits and meditations and character sketches, based on the idea of the sea and the different things it has meant over the centuries.<br /><br />A motley collection of characters come together at the mysterious Almayer Inn, a hotel on the sea front staffed by a family of children, Dira and her four brothers. <br /><br /><blockquote>Perched on the last narrow edge of the world, a stone's throw from the end of the sea, the evening too, the Almayer Inn let the darkness gradually silence the colour of its walls, and of the whole world and the entire ocean. So alone was it there, it seemed a thing forgotten. It was almost as if a procession of inns, of every kind and vintage, has passed by there one day, skirting the coast, when, out of tiredness, one had detached itself from the rest, and, as its traveling companions filed past, it decided to stop on that slight rise, yielding to its own weakness, bowing its head and waiting for the end.</blockquote><br /><br />The hotel fills; the beautiful Madame Deveria, whose husband has sent her to the sea to cure her of adultury; the portrait painter Plasson, who has taken to painting the sea with the sea, standing on the shore with a white canvas and pots of sea water, working as the tide engulfs him until a small boat collects him every day; Elisewin, a 15-year-old girl whose father has sent her to the sea in a last ditch attempt to save her life - she has been dying of fear since she was born; her companion Father Pluche, who thinks one thing but opens his mouth and says quite another; and my pet, my favourite, Professor Bartleboom, who is preparing an encyclopedia about where things end, and every night writes a letter to the love of his life, who he yet to meet but knows one day he shall:<br /><br /><blockquote>Bartleboom is thirty-eight. He thinks that somewhere in this world he will meet a woman who has always been <i>his</i> woman. Every now and then he regrest that detiny has been so stubbornly determined ti make him wait with such indelicate tenacity, but with time he has learned to consider he matter with great serenity. Almost every day, for years now, he has taken pen in hand to write to her. He has no names or addresses to put on envelopes: but he has a life to recount. And to whom, if not her? He thinks that when they meet it will be wonderful to place a mahogany box full of letters in her lap and say to her, "I was waiting for you".<br /><br />She will open the box and slowly, when she so desires, she will read the letter one by one, and as she works her way back up the interminable thread of blue ink she will gather up the years -- the days, the moments -- that that man, before he even met her, had already given to her. Or perhaps, more simply, she will overturn the box and, astonished at that comical snowstorm of letters, she will smile, saying to that man, "You are mad".<br /><br />And she will love him forever.</blockquote><br /><br />'Silk', Alessandro Baricco's best-selling, slim, gently erotic first book, is equally fanciful but is also a genuine fable. 'Ocean Sea' might be for true fans, who can abandon plot for beautiful word pictures. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15132079387142348237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elena T " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elena T " href="/user/show/22966376-elena-t">Elena T </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22966376-elena-t?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer832035564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14877430017062151162">I grabbed this book because it was published the year I was born. I did not read the synopsis, did not look around for reviews or ask my friends if they'd read it, I loved the title and it came out in 1993 so that was enough for me.<br/><br/>That's why, when I started reading, I had absolutely no expectations and when those nasty things are out of the way, it is just so easy to become spellbound by the words flowing through your eyes, into your mind. After the first 20 pages I was absolutely certain that </span>
  <span id="freeText14877430017062151162" style="display:none">I grabbed this book because it was published the year I was born. I did not read the synopsis, did not look around for reviews or ask my friends if they'd read it, I loved the title and it came out in 1993 so that was enough for me.<br /><br />That's why, when I started reading, I had absolutely no expectations and when those nasty things are out of the way, it is just so easy to become spellbound by the words flowing through your eyes, into your mind. After the first 20 pages I was absolutely certain that Ocean Sea was going to join the elite of my favorite books and it most definitely did.<br /><br />Baricco has his own, very experimental and ragged, effortlessly charming, way of writing that you just can't get enough of. Through his colorful and fresh use of language he presents you a puzzle, in this work, a puzzle of people and places and feelings, which is almost a riddle. He gives you bits and pieces without choking you with information, which I loved, because it gives the reader thinking space. You start wondering and trying to connect the dots just before Baricco himself shows you how things are, no, how things MIGHT be, and you find yourself admiring the imaginative wit of this author.<br /><br />I recommend this to anyone who is interested in the very best of contemporary Italian literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14877430017062151162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15068407116224692640" style="display:none">Alessandro Baricco is a wildly popular writer among those who are aquinted with his work and I understand why: Ocean Sea has a very unique writing style and it is strange, highly intelligent and magical at the same time. The dialogue between professor Bartleboom and Dira when they meet at the Almayer inn stands among my favourites in literature. And therefore I regret that for some reason I can't explain his writing style isn't entirely my cup of tea.<br /><br />Perhaps I will appreciate Ocean Sea more in the future. I do think it deserves a higher rating than I have given it right now. This may change one day - I will definitely have to re-read this book as I am aware that I have missed some very important details.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1961606160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7733190382124735806">God knows how much I love Alessandro Baricco but I've been a bit disappointed by this one. The story didn't turn out the way I expected it to be and I thought there would be a deeper relationship between the characters and their relations to the sea... It's clearly a poetic novel, very well written and always pleasant to read but I guess I just expected something else. This is the kind of book you must read several times to notice every detail and to understand every innuendo between the lines.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42216084" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42216084" />
    <a title="Bern" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1614780-bern"><img alt="Bern" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bern" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bern" href="/user/show/1614780-bern">Bern</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42216084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3450678715108814943">The language is stunning. A fragile girl, who can't deal with sharp corners and vivid colors. A priest who writes prayers for the most mundane. A man who writes love letters to someone he has never met. A sailor looking for retribution after a terror at sea. A man who is painting the ocean with evaporating sea water. AMAZING.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Rhwk8Lt5CF+cU9xpCNCmcEj2O0Vyeaot//GgHwL28Kx8Ok0iACfBNzt4aAj/Jayvnqncjbz0JEqkj+auz7UCNg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fVZ9WElpZeh+Dmb34WPFgBqRDFcrkNEAyzGqYJzuQNVHcBSK8jesgNkl0pYWls9fzM7rn+UdX2eQT+zRUa2yTw==')})
  }

Un Dostoevskij lirico, ispirato, comincia a riflettere sulle disillusioni dell’esistenza e dell’amore nell’ultima opera pubblicata prima dell’arresto e della deportazione, esperienze che modificheranno in maniera radicale e definitiva la sua concezione dell’uomo e dell’arte. In questa edizione, al celebre racconto viene affiancata la visione “diurna” di Pietroburgo contenuta nei feuilletons che compongono la Cronaca di Pietroburgo, vero e proprio laboratorio per la scrittura dostoevskiana. Lo stretto legame tra pubblicistica e letteratura, che accompagnerà Dostoevskij negli anni della maturità, viene così a manifestarsi fin quasi dal suo esordio. Il racconto Le notti bianche ha ispirato il film omonimo di Luchino Visconti (1957), con Marcello Mastroianni e Maria Schell, e il film Quattro notti di un sognatore di Robert Bresson <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13311332885884274983">Alessandro Baricco is a popular Italian writer, director, and performer. His novels have been translated into a wide number of languages, and include Lands of Glass, Silk, Ocean Sea, City, and Without Blood. His theatrical monologue, Novecento, was adapted into film, titled The Legend of 1900.<br/><br/>He currently lives in Rome with his wife and two sons.</span>
